<p><b>Earth</b> (<a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>: <span lang="grc" xml:lang="grc">ŒìŒ±·øñŒ±</span>&#160;<span title="Ancient Greek transliteration" class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none"><i>Gaia</i></span>;<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[n 5]</a></sup> Latin: <span lang="la" xml:lang="la"><i>Terra</i></span><sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[25]</a></sup>), otherwise known as the <b>world</b> (especially in <a href="/wiki/Geopolitics" title="Geopolitics">geopolitics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Geography" title="Geography">geography</a>),<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[n 6]</a></sup> is the third <a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">planet</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a> and the only object in the <a href="/wiki/Universe" title="Universe">Universe</a> known to harbor <a href="/wiki/Life" title="Life">life</a>. It is the densest planet in the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a> and the largest of the four <a href="/wiki/Terrestrial_planet" title="Terrestrial planet">terrestrial planets</a>.</p>
<p>According to <a href="/wiki/Radiometric_dating" title="Radiometric dating">radiometric dating</a> and other sources of evidence, Earth <a href="/wiki/Age_of_the_Earth" title="Age of the Earth">formed</a> about 4.54 <a href="/wiki/Bya" title="Bya">billion years ago</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-USGS1997_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USGS1997-33">[27]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[28]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[29]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Gravity_of_Earth" title="Gravity of Earth">Earth's gravity</a> interacts with other objects in space, especially the Sun and the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a>, Earth's only <a href="/wiki/Natural_satellite" title="Natural satellite">natural satellite</a>. During one <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_orbit" title="Earth's orbit">orbit around the Sun</a>, Earth <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_rotation" title="Earth's rotation">rotates about its axis</a> over 365 times; thus, an Earth <a href="/wiki/Year" title="Year">year</a> is about 365.26 days long.<sup id="cite_ref-sidereal_solar_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sidereal_solar-36">[n 7]</a></sup> Earth's <a href="#Axial_tilt_and_seasons">axis of rotation</a> is tilted, producing <a href="/wiki/Season" title="Season">seasonal variations</a> on the planet's surface.<sup id="cite_ref-yoder1995_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-yoder1995-37">[30]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Gravity" title="Gravity">gravitational interaction</a> between the Earth and Moon causes <a href="/wiki/Tide" title="Tide">ocean tides</a>, stabilizes the Earth's orientation on its axis, and gradually slows its rotation.<sup id="cite_ref-aaa428_261_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aaa428_261-38">[31]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Lithosphere#Earth.27s_lithosphere" title="Lithosphere">Earth's lithosphere</a> is divided into several rigid <a href="/wiki/Plate_tectonics" title="Plate tectonics">tectonic plates</a> that migrate across the surface over periods of many millions of years. About 71% of Earth's surface is <a href="/wiki/Water_distribution_on_Earth" title="Water distribution on Earth">covered with water</a>, mostly by its <a href="/wiki/Ocean" title="Ocean">oceans</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[32]</a></sup> The remaining 29% is <a href="/wiki/Land#History_of_land_on_Earth" title="Land">land</a> consisting of <a href="/wiki/Continent" title="Continent">continents</a> and <a href="/wiki/Island" title="Island">islands</a> that together have many lakes, rivers and other sources of water that contribute to the <a href="/wiki/Hydrosphere" title="Hydrosphere">hydrosphere</a>. The majority of Earth's <a href="/wiki/Polar_regions_of_Earth" title="Polar regions of Earth">polar regions</a> are covered in ice, including the <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_ice_sheet" title="Antarctic ice sheet">Antarctic ice sheet</a> and the sea ice of the Arctic ice pack. Earth's interior remains active with a solid iron inner core, a liquid outer core that generates the <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_magnetic_field" title="Earth's magnetic field">Earth's magnetic field</a>, and a convecting <a href="/wiki/Mantle_(geology)" title="Mantle (geology)">mantle</a> that drives plate tectonics.</p>
<p>Within the first billion years of <a href="/wiki/History_of_Earth" title="History of Earth">Earth's history</a>, life appeared in the oceans and began to affect the <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Earth" title="Atmosphere of Earth">Earth's atmosphere</a> and surface, leading to the proliferation of <a href="/wiki/Aerobic_organism" title="Aerobic organism">aerobic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anaerobic_organism" title="Anaerobic organism">anaerobic organisms</a>. Some geological evidence indicates that life may have arisen as much as 4.1 billion years ago. Since then, the combination of Earth's distance from the Sun, physical properties, and <a href="/wiki/Geological_history_of_Earth" title="Geological history of Earth">geological history</a> have allowed life to evolve and thrive.<sup id="cite_ref-AP-20151019_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AP-20151019-40">[33]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-PNAS-20151014-pdf_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PNAS-20151014-pdf-41">[34]</a></sup> In the history of the Earth, <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity" title="Biodiversity">biodiversity</a> has gone through long periods of expansion, occasionally punctuated by <a href="/wiki/Extinction_event" title="Extinction event">mass extinction events</a>. Over 99% of all <a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">species</a><sup id="cite_ref-Book-Biology_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Book-Biology-42">[35]</a></sup> that ever lived on Earth are <a href="/wiki/Extinct" class="mw-redirect" title="Extinct">extinct</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-StearnsStearns2000_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-StearnsStearns2000-43">[36]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NYT-20141108-MJN_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20141108-MJN-44">[37]</a></sup> Estimates of the number of species on Earth today vary widely;<sup id="cite_ref-science_241_4872_1441_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-science_241_4872_1441-45">[38]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-MillerSpoolman2012_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MillerSpoolman2012-46">[39]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NSF-2016002_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NSF-2016002-47">[40]</a></sup> most species have not been described.<sup id="cite_ref-PLoS-20110823_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PLoS-20110823-48">[41]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/World_population" title="World population">Over 7.4 billion humans</a> live on Earth and depend on its <a href="/wiki/Biosphere" title="Biosphere">biosphere</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mineral" title="Mineral">minerals</a> for their survival. Humans have developed diverse <a href="/wiki/Society" title="Society">societies</a> and <a href="/wiki/Culture" title="Culture">cultures</a>; politically, the world has <a href="/wiki/List_of_sovereign_states" title="List of sovereign states">about 200 sovereign states</a>.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Name_and_etymology">Name and etymology</span></h2>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Modern_English" title="Modern English">modern English</a> word <span id="Name"></span><span id="Etymology"></span> <i>Earth</i> developed from a wide variety of <a href="/wiki/Middle_English" title="Middle English">Middle English</a> forms,<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[n 8]</a></sup> which derived from an <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a> noun most often spelled <i><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/eor%C3%B0e" class="extiw" title="wikt:eor√∞e">eor√∞e</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-oedearth_49-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-oedearth-49">[42]</a></sup> It has cognates in every <a href="/wiki/Germanic_languages" title="Germanic languages">Germanic language</a>, and their <a href="/wiki/Proto-Germanic" class="mw-redirect" title="Proto-Germanic">proto-Germanic</a> root has been reconstructed as <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/Appendix:Proto-Germanic/er%C3%BE%C5%8D" class="extiw" title="wikt:Appendix:Proto-Germanic/er√æ≈ç">*<i>er√æ≈ç</i></a>. In its earliest appearances, <i>eor√∞e</i> was already being used to translate the many senses of <a href="/wiki/Latin_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Latin language">Latin</a> <i><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/terra" class="extiw" title="wikt:terra">terra</a></i> and <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Ancient Greek language">Greek</a> <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%CE%B3%E1%BF%86" class="extiw" title="wikt:Œ≥·øÜ">Œ≥·øÜ</a> (<i>gƒì</i>): the ground,<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[n 9]</a></sup> its soil,<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[n 10]</a></sup> dry land,<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[n 11]</a></sup> the human world,<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[n 12]</a></sup> the surface of the world (including the sea),<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[n 13]</a></sup> and the globe itself.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[n 14]</a></sup> As with <a href="/wiki/Terra_(goddess)" class="mw-redirect" title="Terra (goddess)">Terra</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gaia_(goddess)" class="mw-redirect" title="Gaia (goddess)">Gaia</a>, Earth was a <a href="/wiki/Earth_goddess" title="Earth goddess">personified goddess</a> in <a href="/wiki/Germanic_paganism" title="Germanic paganism">Germanic paganism</a>: the <a href="/wiki/Angles" title="Angles">Angles</a> were listed by <a href="/wiki/Tacitus" title="Tacitus">Tacitus</a> as among the <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon_paganism" title="Anglo-Saxon paganism">devotees</a> of <a href="/wiki/Nerthus" title="Nerthus">Nerthus</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[51]</a></sup> and later <a href="/wiki/Norse_mythology" title="Norse mythology">Norse mythology</a> included <a href="/wiki/J%C3%B6r%C3%B0" title="J√∂r√∞">J√∂r√∞</a>, a giantess often given as the mother of <a href="/wiki/Thor" title="Thor">Thor</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-SIMEK179_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SIMEK179-66">[52]</a></sup></p>
<p>Originally, <i>earth</i> was written in lowercase, and from <a href="/wiki/Early_Middle_English" class="mw-redirect" title="Early Middle English">early Middle English</a>, its <a href="/wiki/Definite" class="mw-redirect" title="Definite">definite</a> sense as "the globe" was expressed as <i><a href="/wiki/Definite_article" class="mw-redirect" title="Definite article">the</a> earth</i>. By <a href="/wiki/Early_Modern_English" title="Early Modern English">early Modern English</a>, many nouns were capitalized, and <i>the earth</i> became (and often remained) <i>the Earth</i>, particularly when referenced along with other heavenly bodies. More recently, the name is sometimes simply given as <i>Earth</i>, by analogy with the names of the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">other planets</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-oedearth_49-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-oedearth-49">[42]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Style_guide" title="Style guide">House styles</a> now vary: <a href="/wiki/Oxford_spelling" title="Oxford spelling">Oxford spelling</a> recognizes the lowercase form as the most common, with the capitalized form an acceptable variant. Another convention capitalizes "Earth" when appearing as a name (e.g. "Earth's atmosphere") but writes it in lowercase when preceded by <i>the</i> (e.g. "the atmosphere of the earth"). It almost always appears in lowercase in colloquial expressions such as "what on earth are you doing?"<sup id="cite_ref-oxford_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-oxford-67">[53]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Chronology">Chronology</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Earth" title="History of Earth">History of Earth</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Formation">Formation</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Protoplanetary-disk.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Protoplanetary-disk.jpg/220px-Protoplanetary-disk.jpg" width="220" height="119" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Protoplanetary-disk.jpg/330px-Protoplanetary-disk.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Protoplanetary-disk.jpg/440px-Protoplanetary-disk.jpg 2x" data-file-width="576" data-file-height="312" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Protoplanetary-disk.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Artist's impression of the early Solar System's planetary disk</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The oldest material found in the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a> is dated to <span class="nowrap"><span style="display:none" class="sortkey">7000456720000000000‚ô†</span>4.5672<span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">¬±</span>0.0006&#160;<a href="/wiki/Billion_years_ago" class="mw-redirect" title="Billion years ago">billion years ago</a></span> (Gya).<sup id="cite_ref-bowring_housch1995_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bowring_housch1995-68">[54]</a></sup> By <span class="nowrap"><span style="display:none" class="sortkey">7000454000000000000‚ô†</span>4.54<span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">¬±</span>0.04&#160;Gya</span><sup id="cite_ref-age_earth1_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-age_earth1-69">[55]</a></sup> the primordial Earth had formed. The <a href="/wiki/Formation_and_evolution_of_the_Solar_System" title="Formation and evolution of the Solar System">formation and evolution of Solar System</a> bodies occurred along with the Sun. In theory, a <a href="/wiki/Solar_nebula" class="mw-redirect" title="Solar nebula">solar nebula</a> partitions a volume out of a <a href="/wiki/Molecular_cloud" title="Molecular cloud">molecular cloud</a> by gravitational collapse, which begins to spin and flatten into a <a href="/wiki/Circumstellar_disk" class="mw-redirect" title="Circumstellar disk">circumstellar disk</a>, and then the planets grow out of that disk along with the Sun. A nebula contains gas, ice grains, and <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_dust" title="Cosmic dust">dust</a> (including <a href="/wiki/Primordial_nuclide" title="Primordial nuclide">primordial nuclides</a>). According to <a href="/wiki/Nebular_theory" class="mw-redirect" title="Nebular theory">nebular theory</a>, <a href="/wiki/Planetesimal" title="Planetesimal">planetesimals</a> formed by <a href="/wiki/Accretion_(astrophysics)" title="Accretion (astrophysics)">accretion</a>, with the primordial Earth taking 10‚Äì<span class="nowrap"><span style="display:none" class="sortkey">7001200000000000000‚ô†</span>20&#160;<a href="/wiki/Million_years" class="mw-redirect" title="Million years">million years</a></span> (Ma) to form.<sup id="cite_ref-nature418_6901_949_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nature418_6901_949-70">[56]</a></sup></p>
<p>A subject of on-going research is the formation of the Moon, some 4.53 billion years ago.<sup id="cite_ref-science310_5754_1671_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-science310_5754_1671-71">[57]</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Working_hypothesis" title="Working hypothesis">working hypothesis</a> is that it was formed by accretion from material loosed from Earth after a <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a>-sized object, named <a href="/wiki/Theia_(planet)" title="Theia (planet)">Theia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Giant_impact_hypothesis" class="mw-redirect" title="Giant impact hypothesis">impacted</a> Earth.<sup id="cite_ref-reilly20091022_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reilly20091022-72">[58]</a></sup> In this scenario, the mass of Theia was approximately 10% of that of Earth,<sup id="cite_ref-canup_asphaug2001a_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-canup_asphaug2001a-73">[59]</a></sup> it impacted Earth with a glancing blow,<sup id="cite_ref-canup_asphaug2001b_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-canup_asphaug2001b-74">[60]</a></sup> and some of its mass merged with Earth. Between approximately 4.1 and <span class="nowrap"><span style="display:none" class="sortkey">7000380000000000000‚ô†</span>3.8&#160;Gya</span>, numerous <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">asteroid</a> impacts during the <a href="/wiki/Late_Heavy_Bombardment" title="Late Heavy Bombardment">Late Heavy Bombardment</a> caused significant changes to the greater surface environment of the Moon, and by inference, to that of Earth.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Geological_history">Geological history</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Geological_history_of_Earth" title="Geological history of Earth">Geological history of Earth</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Thunderstorm_Arch_(5740783227).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Thunderstorm_Arch_%285740783227%29.jpg/220px-Thunderstorm_Arch_%285740783227%29.jpg" width="220" height="150" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Thunderstorm_Arch_%285740783227%29.jpg/330px-Thunderstorm_Arch_%285740783227%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Thunderstorm_Arch_%285740783227%29.jpg/440px-Thunderstorm_Arch_%285740783227%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1318" data-file-height="900" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Thunderstorm_Arch_(5740783227).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A natural stone arch, showing rock layers</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Earth's atmosphere and oceans were formed by <a href="/wiki/Volcano" title="Volcano">volcanic activity</a> and <a href="/wiki/Outgassing" title="Outgassing">outgassing</a> that included water vapor. The <a href="/wiki/Origin_of_the_world%27s_oceans" class="mw-redirect" title="Origin of the world's oceans">origin of the world's oceans</a> was condensation augmented by water and ice delivered by asteroids, <a href="/wiki/Protoplanet" title="Protoplanet">protoplanets</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Comet" title="Comet">comets</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-watersource_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-watersource-75">[61]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Faint_young_Sun_paradox" title="Faint young Sun paradox">this model</a>, atmospheric "greenhouse gases" kept the oceans from freezing when the newly forming Sun had only 70% of its <a href="/wiki/Solar_luminosity" title="Solar luminosity">current luminosity</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-asp2002_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-asp2002-76">[62]</a></sup> By <span class="nowrap"><span style="display:none" class="sortkey">7000350000000000000‚ô†</span>3.5&#160;Gya</span>, <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_magnetic_field" title="Earth's magnetic field">Earth's magnetic field</a> was established, which helped prevent the atmosphere from being stripped away by the <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">solar wind</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-physorg20100304_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-physorg20100304-77">[63]</a></sup></p>
<p>A crust formed when the molten outer layer of Earth cooled <a href="/wiki/Phase_transition" title="Phase transition">to form</a> a solid. The two models<sup id="cite_ref-williams_santosh2004_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-williams_santosh2004-78">[64]</a></sup> that explain land mass propose either a steady growth to the present-day forms<sup id="cite_ref-science164_1229_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-science164_1229-79">[65]</a></sup> or, more likely, a rapid growth<sup id="cite_ref-tp322_19_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tp322_19-80">[66]</a></sup> early in Earth history<sup id="cite_ref-rg6_175_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rg6_175-81">[67]</a></sup> followed by a long-term steady continental area.<sup id="cite_ref-science310_5756_1947_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-science310_5756_1947-82">[68]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-jaes23_799_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jaes23_799-83">[69]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ajes38_613_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ajes38_613-84">[70]</a></sup> Continents formed by <a href="/wiki/Plate_tectonics" title="Plate tectonics">plate tectonics</a>, a process ultimately driven by the continuous loss of heat from Earth's interior. On <a href="/wiki/Geologic_time_scale" title="Geologic time scale">time scales</a> lasting hundreds of millions of years, the <a href="/wiki/Supercontinent" title="Supercontinent">supercontinents</a> have assembled and broken apart. Roughly <span class="nowrap"><span style="display:none" class="sortkey">7002750000000000000‚ô†</span>750&#160;Mya</span> (million years ago), one of the earliest known supercontinents, <a href="/wiki/Rodinia" title="Rodinia">Rodinia</a>, began to break apart. The continents later recombined to form <a href="/wiki/Pannotia" title="Pannotia">Pannotia</a>, 600‚Äì<span class="nowrap"><span style="display:none" class="sortkey">7002540000000000000‚ô†</span>540&#160;Mya</span>, then finally <a href="/wiki/Pangaea" title="Pangaea">Pangaea</a>, which also broke apart <span class="nowrap"><span style="display:none" class="sortkey">7002180000000000000‚ô†</span>180&#160;Mya</span>.<sup id="cite_ref-as92_324_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-as92_324-85">[71]</a></sup></p>
<p>The present pattern of <a href="/wiki/Ice_age" title="Ice age">ice ages</a> began about <span class="nowrap"><span style="display:none" class="sortkey">7001400000000000000‚ô†</span>40&#160;Mya</span> and then intensified during the <a href="/wiki/Pleistocene" title="Pleistocene">Pleistocene</a> about <span class="nowrap"><span style="display:none" class="sortkey">7000300000000000000‚ô†</span>3&#160;Mya</span>. High-<a href="/wiki/Latitude" title="Latitude">latitude</a> regions have since undergone repeated cycles of glaciation and thaw, repeating about every 40,000‚Äì<span class="nowrap"><span style="display:none" class="sortkey">7012315576000000000‚ô†</span>100<span style="margin-left:.25em;">000</span>&#160;years</span>. The last continental glaciation ended 10,000&#160;years ago.<sup id="cite_ref-psc_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-psc-86">[72]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Origin_of_life_and_evolution">Origin of life and evolution</span></h3>
<div id="Container" class="toccolours searchaux" style="text-align: left; padding:0.5em 0 0 0; border-style:solid; float:right;clear:right; padding:0 0.5em; margin:0.3em 0 0.8em 1.4em; overflow: hidden;">

<div id="Legend" class="toccolours" style="margin-left:3.1em; border-style:none; float:left; clear:both;"></div>
<div id="Caption" class="toccolours" style="margin:0 0.5em; float:left; padding:0 auto; border-style:none; clear:both; text-align:center; width:20.5em"><a href="/wiki/Cartesian_coordinate_system#Two_dimensions" title="Cartesian coordinate system">Axis scale</a>: <a href="/wiki/Geologic_time_scale" title="Geologic time scale">millions of years</a>.<br />
<small><span style="font:bold 10px Arial;display:inline;background-color:rgba(255,165,0,0.5);padding:0px 4px 0px 4px;"><span style="color:black;">Orange labels:</span> <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_glaciation" title="Timeline of glaciation"><span style="color:black;">known <i><b>ice ages</b></i>.</span></a></span></small><br />
<small>Also see: <i><a href="/wiki/Template:Human_timeline" title="Template:Human timeline">Human timeline</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Template:Nature_timeline" title="Template:Nature timeline">Nature timeline</a></i></small></div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Abiogenesis" title="Abiogenesis">Abiogenesis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_history_of_life" title="Evolutionary history of life">Evolutionary history of life</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:PhylogeneticTree,_Woese_1990.PNG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/PhylogeneticTree%2C_Woese_1990.PNG/220px-PhylogeneticTree%2C_Woese_1990.PNG" width="220" height="150" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/PhylogeneticTree%2C_Woese_1990.PNG/330px-PhylogeneticTree%2C_Woese_1990.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/PhylogeneticTree%2C_Woese_1990.PNG/440px-PhylogeneticTree%2C_Woese_1990.PNG 2x" data-file-width="1842" data-file-height="1257" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:PhylogeneticTree,_Woese_1990.PNG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Speculative <a href="/wiki/Phylogenetic_tree" title="Phylogenetic tree">phylogenetic tree</a> of life on Earth based on <a href="/wiki/RRNA" class="mw-redirect" title="RRNA">rRNA</a> analysis</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Chemical_reaction" title="Chemical reaction">Chemical reactions</a> led to the first self-replicating molecules about four billion years ago. A half billion years later, the <a href="/wiki/Last_universal_common_ancestor" title="Last universal common ancestor">last common ancestor of all life</a> arose.<sup id="cite_ref-sa282_6_90_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sa282_6_90-87">[73]</a></sup> The evolution of <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthesis</a> allowed the Sun's energy to be harvested directly by life forms. The resultant <a href="/wiki/Molecular_oxygen" class="mw-redirect" title="Molecular oxygen">molecular oxygen</a> (O<sub>2</sub>) accumulated in the atmosphere and due to interaction with ultraviolet solar radiation, formed a protective <a href="/wiki/Ozone_layer" title="Ozone layer">ozone layer</a> (O<sub>3</sub>) in the upper atmosphere.<sup id="cite_ref-NYT-20131003_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20131003-88">[74]</a></sup> The incorporation of smaller cells within larger ones resulted in the <a href="/wiki/Endosymbiotic_theory" class="mw-redirect" title="Endosymbiotic theory">development of complex cells</a> called <a href="/wiki/Eukaryotes" class="mw-redirect" title="Eukaryotes">eukaryotes</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-jas22_3_225_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jas22_3_225-89">[75]</a></sup> True multicellular organisms formed as cells within <a href="/wiki/Colony_(biology)" title="Colony (biology)">colonies</a> became increasingly specialized. Aided by the absorption of harmful <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet_radiation" class="mw-redirect" title="Ultraviolet radiation">ultraviolet radiation</a> by the ozone layer, life colonized Earth's surface.<sup id="cite_ref-burton20021129_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-burton20021129-90">[76]</a></sup> Among the earliest <a href="/wiki/Fossil" title="Fossil">fossil</a> evidence for <a href="/wiki/Life" title="Life">life</a> is <a href="/wiki/Microbial_mat" title="Microbial mat">microbial mat</a> fossils found in 3.48 billion-year-old <a href="/wiki/Sandstone" title="Sandstone">sandstone</a> in <a href="/wiki/Western_Australia" title="Western Australia">Western Australia</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Origin1_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Origin1-91">[77]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Origin2_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Origin2-92">[78]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-RavenJohnson2002_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RavenJohnson2002-93">[79]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AP-20131113_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AP-20131113-94">[80]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AST-20131108_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AST-20131108-95">[81]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Biogenic_substance" title="Biogenic substance">biogenic</a> <a href="/wiki/Graphite" title="Graphite">graphite</a> found in 3.7 billion-year-old <a href="/wiki/Metasediment" title="Metasediment">metasedimentary rocks</a> in <a href="/wiki/Western_Greenland" class="mw-redirect" title="Western Greenland">Western Greenland</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-NG-20131208_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NG-20131208-96">[82]</a></sup> remains of <a href="/wiki/Biotic_material" title="Biotic material">biotic material</a> found in 4.1 billion-year-old rocks in Western Australia.<sup id="cite_ref-AP-20151019_40-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AP-20151019-40">[33]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-PNAS-20151014-pdf_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PNAS-20151014-pdf-41">[34]</a></sup></p>
<p>During the <a href="/wiki/Neoproterozoic" title="Neoproterozoic">Neoproterozoic</a>, <span class="nowrap"><span style="display:none" class="sortkey">7002750000000000000‚ô†</span>750 to 580&#160;Mya</span>, much of Earth might have been covered in ice. This hypothesis has been termed "<a href="/wiki/Snowball_Earth" title="Snowball Earth">Snowball Earth</a>", and it is of particular interest because it preceded the <a href="/wiki/Cambrian_explosion" title="Cambrian explosion">Cambrian explosion</a>, when multicellular life forms significantly increased in complexity.<sup id="cite_ref-kirschvink1992_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kirschvink1992-97">[83]</a></sup> Following the Cambrian explosion, <span class="nowrap"><span style="display:none" class="sortkey">7002535000000000000‚ô†</span>535&#160;Mya</span>, there have been five <a href="/wiki/Extinction_event" title="Extinction event">major mass extinctions</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-sci215_4539_1501_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sci215_4539_1501-98">[84]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Cretaceous%E2%80%93Tertiary_extinction_event" class="mw-redirect" title="Cretaceous‚ÄìTertiary extinction event">most recent such event</a> was <span class="nowrap"><span style="display:none" class="sortkey">7001660000000000000‚ô†</span>66&#160;Mya</span>, when <a href="/wiki/Chicxulub_impactor" title="Chicxulub impactor">an asteroid impact</a> triggered the extinction of the non-<a href="/wiki/Bird" title="Bird">avian</a> <a href="/wiki/Dinosaur" title="Dinosaur">dinosaurs</a> and other large reptiles, but spared some small animals such as <a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">mammals</a>, which then resembled <a href="/wiki/Shrew" title="Shrew">shrews</a>. Over the past <span class="nowrap"><span style="display:none" class="sortkey">7015208280160000000‚ô†</span>66&#160;<a href="/wiki/Annum" class="mw-redirect" title="Annum">Ma</a></span>, mammalian life has diversified, and several million years ago an African ape-like animal such as <i><a href="/wiki/Orrorin_tugenensis" class="mw-redirect" title="Orrorin tugenensis">Orrorin tugenensis</a></i> gained the ability to stand upright.<sup id="cite_ref-gould1994_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gould1994-99">[85]</a></sup> This facilitated tool use and encouraged communication that provided the nutrition and stimulation needed for a larger brain, which allowed the <a href="/wiki/Human_evolution" title="Human evolution">evolution of humans</a>. The <a href="/wiki/History_of_agriculture" title="History of agriculture">development of agriculture</a>, and then <a href="/wiki/List_of_ancient_civilizations" class="mw-redirect" title="List of ancient civilizations">civilization</a>, led to humans having an <a href="/wiki/Human_impact_on_the_environment" title="Human impact on the environment">influence on Earth</a> and the nature and quantity of other life forms that continues today.<sup id="cite_ref-bgsa119_1_140_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bgsa119_1_140-100">[86]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Future">Future</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Future_of_Earth" title="Future of Earth">Future of Earth</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Global_catastrophic_risk" title="Global catastrophic risk">Global catastrophic risk</a></div>
<p>Earth's expected long-term future is closely tied to that of the Sun. Over the next <span class="nowrap"><span style="display:none" class="sortkey">7016347133600000000‚ô†</span>1.1&#160;Ga</span>, solar luminosity will increase by 10%, and over the next <span class="nowrap"><span style="display:none" class="sortkey">7017110451600000000‚ô†</span>3.5&#160;Ga</span> by 40%.<sup id="cite_ref-sun_future_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sun_future-101">[87]</a></sup> The Earth's increasing surface temperature will accelerate the <a href="/wiki/Inorganic" class="mw-redirect" title="Inorganic">inorganic</a> <a href="/wiki/Carbon_cycle" title="Carbon cycle">CO<sub>2</sub> cycle</a>, reducing its concentration to levels lethally low for plants (<span class="nowrap"><span style="display:none" class="sortkey">6995099999999999999‚ô†</span>10&#160;<a href="/wiki/Parts_per_million" class="mw-redirect" title="Parts per million">ppm</a></span> for <a href="/wiki/C4_carbon_fixation" title="C4 carbon fixation">C4 photosynthesis</a>) in approximately 500‚Äì<span class="nowrap"><span style="display:none" class="sortkey">7016284018400000000‚ô†</span>900&#160;Ma</span>.<sup id="cite_ref-britt2000_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-britt2000-102">[88]</a></sup> The lack of vegetation will result in the loss of oxygen in the atmosphere, and animal life will become extinct.<sup id="cite_ref-ward_brownlee2002_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ward_brownlee2002-103">[89]</a></sup> After another billion years all surface water will have disappeared<sup id="cite_ref-carrington_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-carrington-104">[90]</a></sup> and the mean global temperature will reach <span class="nowrap"><span style="display:none" class="sortkey">7002343150000000000‚ô†</span>70&#160;<a href="/wiki/Celsius" title="Celsius">¬∞C</a></span><sup id="cite_ref-ward_brownlee2002_103-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ward_brownlee2002-103">[89]</a></sup> (<span class="nowrap"><span style="display:none" class="sortkey">7002343150000000000‚ô†</span>158&#160;<a href="/wiki/Fahrenheit" title="Fahrenheit">¬∞F</a></span>). From that point, the Earth is expected to be habitable for another <span class="nowrap"><span style="display:none" class="sortkey">7016157788000000000‚ô†</span>500&#160;Ma</span>,<sup id="cite_ref-britt2000_102-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-britt2000-102">[88]</a></sup> possibly up to <span class="nowrap"><span style="display:none" class="sortkey">7016725824800000000‚ô†</span>2.3&#160;Ga</span> if nitrogen is removed from the atmosphere.<sup id="cite_ref-pnas1_24_9576_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pnas1_24_9576-105">[91]</a></sup> Even if the Sun were eternal and stable, 27% of the water in the modern oceans will descend to the <a href="/wiki/Mantle_(geology)" title="Mantle (geology)">mantle</a> in one billion years, due to reduced steam venting from mid-ocean ridges.<sup id="cite_ref-hess5_4_569_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hess5_4_569-106">[92]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Sun will <a href="/wiki/Stellar_evolution" title="Stellar evolution">evolve</a> to become a <a href="/wiki/Red_giant" title="Red giant">red giant</a> in about <span class="nowrap"><span style="display:none" class="sortkey">7017157788000000000‚ô†</span>5&#160;Ga</span>. Models predict that the Sun will expand to roughly 1&#160;AU (150,000,000&#160;km), which is about 250 times its present radius.<sup id="cite_ref-sun_future_101-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sun_future-101">[87]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-sun_future_schroder_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sun_future_schroder-107">[93]</a></sup> Earth's fate is less clear. As a red giant, the Sun will lose roughly 30% of its mass, so, without tidal effects, Earth will move to an orbit 1.7 AU from the Sun when the star reaches its maximum radius. Most, if not all, remaining life will be destroyed by the Sun's increased luminosity (peaking at about 5,000 times its present level).<sup id="cite_ref-sun_future_101-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sun_future-101">[87]</a></sup> A 2008 simulation indicates that Earth's orbit will eventually decay due to <a href="/wiki/Tidal_acceleration" title="Tidal acceleration">tidal effects</a> and drag, causing it to enter the Sun's atmosphere and be vaporized.<sup id="cite_ref-sun_future_schroder_107-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sun_future_schroder-107">[93]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_characteristics">Physical characteristics</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Shape">Shape</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Earth2014shape_SouthAmerica_small.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Earth2014shape_SouthAmerica_small.jpg/220px-Earth2014shape_SouthAmerica_small.jpg" width="220" height="200" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Earth2014shape_SouthAmerica_small.jpg/330px-Earth2014shape_SouthAmerica_small.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Earth2014shape_SouthAmerica_small.jpg/440px-Earth2014shape_SouthAmerica_small.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1500" data-file-height="1362" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Earth2014shape_SouthAmerica_small.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Shape of planet Earth. Shown are distances between surface relief and the geocentre. The South American Andes summits are visible as elevated areas. Data from the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Earth2014&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Earth2014 (page does not exist)">Earth2014</a><sup id="cite_ref-Earth2014_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Earth2014-108">[94]</a></sup> global relief model.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Figure_of_the_Earth" title="Figure of the Earth">Figure of the Earth</a></div>
<p>The shape of Earth is approximately <a href="/wiki/Oblate_spheroid" class="mw-redirect" title="Oblate spheroid">oblate spheroidal</a>. Due to <a href="/wiki/Rotation" title="Rotation">rotation</a>, the Earth is flattened along the geographic axis and <a href="/wiki/Equatorial_bulge" title="Equatorial bulge">bulging</a> around the <a href="/wiki/Equator" title="Equator">equator</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-milbert_smith96_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-milbert_smith96-109">[95]</a></sup> The diameter of the Earth at the equator is 43 kilometres (27&#160;mi) larger than the <a href="/wiki/Geographical_pole" title="Geographical pole">pole</a>-to-pole diameter.<sup id="cite_ref-ngdc2006_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ngdc2006-110">[96]</a></sup> Thus the point on the surface farthest from Earth's <a href="/wiki/Center_of_mass" title="Center of mass">center of mass</a> is the summit of the equatorial <a href="/wiki/Chimborazo_(volcano)" class="mw-redirect" title="Chimborazo (volcano)">Chimborazo</a> volcano in <a href="/wiki/Ecuador" title="Ecuador">Ecuador</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ps20_5_16_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ps20_5_16-111">[97]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-lancet365_9462_831_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lancet365_9462_831-112">[98]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-tall_tales_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tall_tales-113">[99]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-The_.27Highest.27_Spot_on_Earth_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_.27Highest.27_Spot_on_Earth-114">[100]</a></sup> The average diameter of the reference spheroid is 12,742 kilometres (7,918&#160;mi). Local <a href="/wiki/Topography" title="Topography">topography</a> deviates from this idealized spheroid, although on a global scale these deviations are small compared to Earth's radius: The maximum deviation of only 0.17% is at the <a href="/wiki/Mariana_Trench" title="Mariana Trench">Mariana Trench</a> (10,911 metres (35,797&#160;ft) below local sea level), whereas <a href="/wiki/Mount_Everest" title="Mount Everest">Mount Everest</a> (8,848 metres (29,029&#160;ft) above local sea level) represents a deviation of 0.14%.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">[n 15]</a></sup></p>

<td style="text-align: center;">TiO<sub>2</sub></td>
<td style="text-align: right;">0.7%</td>
<td style="text-align: right;">1.4%</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/Phosphorus_pentoxide" title="Phosphorus pentoxide">phosphorus pentoxide</a></td>
<td style="text-align: center;">P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub></td>

<th style="text-align: right;">99.9%</th>
</tr>
</table>
<p><a href="/wiki/Earth_mass" title="Earth mass">Earth's mass</a> is approximately <span class="nowrap"><span style="display:none" class="sortkey">7024597000000000000‚ô†</span>5.97<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">√ó</span>10<sup>24</sup>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Kilogram" title="Kilogram">kg</a></span> (5,970 <a href="/wiki/Yottagram" class="mw-redirect" title="Yottagram">Yg</a>). It is composed mostly of <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a> (32.1%), <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> (30.1%), <a href="/wiki/Silicon" title="Silicon">silicon</a> (15.1%), <a href="/wiki/Magnesium" title="Magnesium">magnesium</a> (13.9%), <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a> (2.9%), <a href="/wiki/Nickel" title="Nickel">nickel</a> (1.8%), <a href="/wiki/Calcium" title="Calcium">calcium</a> (1.5%), and <a href="/wiki/Aluminium" title="Aluminium">aluminium</a> (1.4%), with the remaining 1.2% consisting of trace amounts of other elements. Due to <a href="/wiki/Mass_segregation" class="mw-redirect" title="Mass segregation">mass segregation</a>, the core region is estimated to be primarily composed of iron (88.8%), with smaller amounts of nickel (5.8%), sulfur (4.5%), and less than 1% trace elements.<sup id="cite_ref-pnas71_12_6973_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pnas71_12_6973-118">[103]</a></sup></p>
<p>The most common rock constituents of the crust are nearly all oxides: chlorine, sulfur, and fluorine are the important exceptions to this and their total amount in any rock is usually much less than 1%. Over 99% of the crust is composed of 11 oxides, principally silica, alumina, iron oxides, lime, magnesia, potash, and soda.<sup id="cite_ref-brown_mussett1981_117-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-brown_mussett1981-117">[102]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-pnas71_12_6973_118-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pnas71_12_6973-118">[103]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">[104]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Internal_structure">Internal structure</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Structure_of_the_Earth" title="Structure of the Earth">Structure of the Earth</a></div>
<p>Earth's interior, like that of the other terrestrial planets, is divided into layers by their <a href="/wiki/Chemical" class="mw-redirect" title="Chemical">chemical</a> or physical (<a href="/wiki/Rheology" title="Rheology">rheological</a>) properties. The outer layer is a chemically distinct <a href="/wiki/Silicate_minerals" title="Silicate minerals">silicate</a> solid crust, which is underlain by a highly <a href="/wiki/Viscous" class="mw-redirect" title="Viscous">viscous</a> solid mantle. The crust is separated from the mantle by the <a href="/wiki/Mohorovi%C4%8Di%C4%87_discontinuity" title="Mohoroviƒçiƒá discontinuity">Mohoroviƒçiƒá discontinuity</a>. The thickness of the crust varies from about <span class="nowrap"><span style="display:none" class="sortkey">7003600000000000000‚ô†</span>6&#160;<a href="/wiki/Kilometre" title="Kilometre">km</a></span> (kilometers) under the oceans to 30‚Äì50&#160;km for the continents. The crust and the cold, rigid, top of the <a href="/wiki/Upper_mantle" class="mw-redirect" title="Upper mantle">upper mantle</a> are collectively known as the lithosphere, and it is of the lithosphere that the tectonic plates are composed. Beneath the lithosphere is the <a href="/wiki/Asthenosphere" title="Asthenosphere">asthenosphere</a>, a relatively low-viscosity layer on which the lithosphere rides. Important changes in crystal structure within the mantle occur at 410 and <span class="nowrap"><span style="display:none" class="sortkey">7005660000000000000‚ô†</span>660&#160;km</span> below the surface, spanning a <a href="/wiki/Transition_zone_(Earth)" title="Transition zone (Earth)">transition zone</a> that separates the upper and lower mantle. Beneath the mantle, an extremely low viscosity liquid <a href="/wiki/Outer_core" title="Outer core">outer core</a> lies above a solid <a href="/wiki/Inner_core" title="Inner core">inner core</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-tanimoto_ahrens1995_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tanimoto_ahrens1995-120">[105]</a></sup> The Earth's inner core might rotate at a slightly higher <a href="/wiki/Angular_velocity" title="Angular velocity">angular velocity</a> than the remainder of the planet, advancing by 0.1‚Äì0.5¬∞ per year.<sup id="cite_ref-science309_5739_1313_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-science309_5739_1313-121">[106]</a></sup> The radius of the inner core is about one fifth of that of Earth.</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Heat">Heat</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_internal_heat_budget" title="Earth's internal heat budget">Earth's internal heat budget</a></div>
<p>Earth's <a href="/wiki/Internal_heat" class="mw-redirect" title="Internal heat">internal heat</a> comes from a combination of residual heat from <a href="/wiki/Planetary_accretion" class="mw-redirect" title="Planetary accretion">planetary accretion</a> (about 20%) and heat produced through <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_decay" title="Radioactive decay">radioactive decay</a> (80%).<sup id="cite_ref-turcotte_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-turcotte-126">[109]</a></sup> The major heat-producing <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">isotopes</a> within Earth are <a href="/wiki/Potassium-40" title="Potassium-40">potassium-40</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uranium-238" title="Uranium-238">uranium-238</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Thorium-232" class="mw-redirect" title="Thorium-232">thorium-232</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-sanders20031210_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sanders20031210-127">[110]</a></sup> At the center, the temperature may be up to 6,000&#160;¬∞C (10,830&#160;¬∞F),<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">[111]</a></sup> and the pressure could reach 360&#160;<a href="/wiki/GPa" class="mw-redirect" title="GPa">GPa</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ptrsl360_1795_1227_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ptrsl360_1795_1227-129">[112]</a></sup> Because much of the heat is provided by radioactive decay, scientists postulate that early in Earth's history, before isotopes with short half-lives were depleted, Earth's heat production was much higher. At approximately <span class="nowrap"><span style="display:none" class="sortkey">7016946728000000000‚ô†</span>3&#160;<a href="/wiki/Year#SI_prefix_multipliers" title="Year">Ga</a></span>, twice the present-day heat would have been produced, increasing the rates of <a href="/wiki/Mantle_convection" title="Mantle convection">mantle convection</a> and plate tectonics, and allowing the production of uncommon igneous rocks such as <a href="/wiki/Komatiites" class="mw-redirect" title="Komatiites">komatiites</a> that are rarely formed today.<sup id="cite_ref-turcotte_126-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-turcotte-126">[109]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-epsl121_1_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-epsl121_1-130">[113]</a></sup></p>
<table class="wikitable" style="text-align:center;">

<p>The mean heat loss from Earth is <span class="nowrap">87 mW m<sup>‚àí2</sup></span>, for a global heat loss of <span class="nowrap">4.42 √ó 10<sup>13</sup> W</span>.<sup id="cite_ref-jg31_3_267_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jg31_3_267-132">[115]</a></sup> A portion of the core's thermal energy is transported toward the crust by <a href="/wiki/Mantle_plume" title="Mantle plume">mantle plumes</a>, a form of convection consisting of upwellings of higher-temperature rock. These plumes can produce <a href="/wiki/Hotspot_(geology)" title="Hotspot (geology)">hotspots</a> and <a href="/wiki/Flood_basalt" title="Flood basalt">flood basalts</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-science246_4926_103_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-science246_4926_103-133">[116]</a></sup> More of the heat in Earth is lost through plate tectonics, by mantle upwelling associated with <a href="/wiki/Mid-ocean_ridge" title="Mid-ocean ridge">mid-ocean ridges</a>. The final major mode of heat loss is through conduction through the lithosphere, the majority of which occurs under the oceans because the crust there is much thinner than that of the continents.<sup id="cite_ref-heat_loss_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-heat_loss-134">[117]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:right;"></div>
